# Bullinger's Apocalypse Sermons

*Bullinger, Heinrich, 1504-1575.*

---

## SERMONS OF HENRI BULLINGER, upon the Apocalypse revealed to the holy Apostle and Evangelist S. John.

## Sermon 1: ☞ Of the author of the book of Apocalypse, of the argument and parts thereof: Finally of the sundry use, and most profitable commodity of the same.

### Paragraph 1

The prophets of God of the old Testament were God’s messengers to the people, expositors of Moses or God’s law, and even ecclesiastical preachers, who applied the doctrine taken out of the law of God to the people and times wherein they lived for the edifying of congregations. And they all with one accord have chiefly handled two things in their sermons. For first, they have reproved the corrupt manners of all states in their time, alleging the rule and prescription of God’s law; and exhorting all men to repentance unto God most acceptable. And to many that were uncurable they threatened all kinds of plagues, which they setting forth with all beauty of speech, showed them plainly to be seen with the eye, if they apply them so might be made afraid, and healed. Secondly, they promised and set forth by the mouth of God, the Lord Christ, the true Messiah: Whom also they described lively, and with all his holy church, teaching the faith in Christ, and oath what good things are prepared for the faithful in Christ; and also what be the true duties of piety and godliness. Neither have they concealed such things as were needful to be known of Antichrist: admonishing us most diligently that we should beware of that wolf, or rather the most deep pit of all abominations, and that we should stand fast in the sincere faith of Christ. &c.

### Paragraph 2

I have taken upon me, through the help of God and your prayers, to expound unto you the Apocalypse,[note 1.2] which is a doctrine concerning the matters of Christ's church revealed from heaven by Christ in glory, and a summary of all godly religion, an exposition and brief declaration of the Prophets, and a prophecy of the New Testament and history of the church.

### Paragraph 3

However, since this book is despised by both good and learned men, and because most people are firmly convinced that it is an unprofitable labor, I will speak here somewhat about this matter.

### Paragraph 4

First, many abhor this book for this cause only that it is full of visions, types, and figures, for they suppose this does not accord with the Evangelical and Apostolic doctrine of the New Testament. But by the same reasoning, a portion of Daniel should be cast away. Nevertheless, this is commended to us by Christ himself in Matthew. The greatest matter of all, namely the calling of the Gentiles, was shown to Saint Peter by a vision, as appears in the Acts.

### Paragraph 5

And the prophet Joel also said how the people of the New Testament should see visions. And so does the blessed Apostle Peter expound the same place in the Acts of the Apostles, speaking of the people of the New Testament. And our Savior Christ in the Gospel, proposed and declared to the people many mysteries by parables, and in a manner by feigned fables, as they call them. And how much do you think these visions, types, and figures of John differ from the same? This kind of speaking does not darken matters, but makes them plain. And it contributes much to the efficacy and clarity, and to the confirming of the memory. For by this means, matters are not only declared with words and heard with the ears, but are set forth also to be seen by the eyes, and after a sort are fixed in the memory.

Many for this cause attribute much to painting. But I suppose that I may much more rightly attribute very much to this manner of speaking and teaching, where the matter is uttered, not by a colored, dull, and dead painting, but as it were with a speaking and lively manner set forth to be seen by the eyes. Which is therefore proposed, that men might rightly and exactly understand the same. Although therefore this whole book in a manner consists of visions and figures, yet shall we, through the inspiration of God’s grace, show in our exposition that it pertains to the perspicuity and plainness, and not to the obscuring or darkening of most high and godly matters. I will bring my exposition out of the very scriptures, by comparing and joining thereunto the rule of faith and charity. I will search out the circumstances, the things that follow and go before. I will bring similitudes and dissimilitudes. I will add also thereunto the experience of things, and the faith of histories. Which manner of expounding the scriptures all interpreters have always granted to be sound and true.

If better things shall be revealed to others, I will gladly, after the precept of the apostle, give place unto my betters. For I offer these my doings to be weighed by the godly, upon this condition, that they should try all things, and that which they shall find to be good, to hold fast.

### Paragraph 6

Secondly they object, that as well new men as old, of no small authority, have both doubted of this book, and of the author thereof, and also have contemned it, as full of fables, and unworthy to be reckoned canonical. Let those that so think give me the same liberty, which I desire them, which they usurp themselves, and think it lawful. For if the book of the Apocalypse should therefore seem worthy to be contemned, for that some notable men, both old and new, have doubted of the authority hereof: Why may it not recover his authority again, if I shew that the best doctors of the church both old and new, have had a right good opinion of this book? And here to the intent I would dissemble nothing at all, I am not ignorant that Doctor Martin Luther, a man right notably learned, in his first edition of the New Testament in Dutch, with a sharp preface set before, hath stuck this book as it were with a dagger. However, it grieved good and well learned men, who found lack both of wit and modesty in that judgment. The same man therefore weighing all things more uprightly and diligently, what time he corrected his Dutch Bible. My worshipful master seems also not to have set very much by his book, and to have ascribed it not to John the Apostle, but to John who they called a divine. But herein there is no doubt but that he followed plainly Erasmus of Rotterdam, who is his annotations upon the New Testament. In all the Greek copies, he says, that I have seen, the title was not of John the Apostle, but of John the divine. Erasmus adds that amongst the Greeks and certain old writers, men doubted of this author, which thing he declares by the testimonies of Eusebius and Jerome, of whose opinion shall be spoken straightways. But the exemplar or Spanish copy which is set forth after the faith of the most ancient and approved Greeks, exhibiteth to us such a title of this book: That is, the Apocalypse of the holy Apostle and Evangelist St. John Divine. For the ancient writers say, how St. John the Apostle and Evangelist, for his excellent writing of the Son of God, was commonly called, Divine. Whereof it follows, than this title doth attribute and not take from St. John this book. Certainly Arethas was also, a Greek author, a bishop of Caesarea: Of the ancients, says he, certain have plucked this Apocalypse from the tongue of that well-beloved John, ascribing it to another: but it is not so. For that same Gregory, which as well as he, is called a Divine, accounts this amongst those scriptures, which utterly want a suspicion of counterfeiting: saying, as the Apocalypse of St. John teaches me. And the same man a little after: But though this book was written by the mouth of the Holy Ghost, St. Basil, Cyril, Papias and Hippolytus, fathers of the church, are men to be credited. Thus saith he. What shall we say that Erasmus confesses, that the consent of the world, an authority of the church to be of such force with him, that he dare not refuse this book?

### Paragraph 7

Let us hear now the judgment of that most excellent man, Dr. John Oecolampadius, the most faithful pillar of the church of Basel, and excellently learned in prophetic matters and in all the canonical scriptures, concerning this book, which he left us written in chapter 12 of his second book of commentaries on Daniel. But St. Jerome, the paraphraser or interpreter of the Prophets, says—[he expresses how much he attributes to this author]—that it is to be wondered why some, with such hasty judgment, reject him as a dreamer, a madman, and an unprofitable writer to the church. Nevertheless, he proposes and sets forth many of the most secret and hidden things of the old covenant and of the Prophets. But those great men betray what a sense of their own importance they have. Their judgments I would rather scorn as profane than I would cast away such a treasure. I could here bring forth good testimonies of other new writers, but I hasten to the judgments of the ancient fathers.

### Paragraph 8

The earliest of all after the Apostles, Justin and Irenaeus, the noble martyrs of Christ, attribute this book to John the Apostle. For Eusebius, in chapter 18 of the fourth book of the Ecclesiastical History, states that Justin mentions the Apocalypse of John, saying plainly that it is the Apostle’s. Jerome also writes in the life of blessed Justin that Justin explained the Apocalypse of John, but that explanation does not survive to any great extent as far as I know. The same author writes that Irenaeus presented the Apocalypse of John with a commentary, which is also unavailable. He himself, said to have lived around the year of our Lord 160, plainly testifies in the fifth book against the Valentinians that this revelation was shown to John the Apostle a little before his time. We cite certain words of his in chapter 13 of this book. Tertullian, who lived around the year of our Lord two hundred and twenty, in the fourth book against Marcion, although he says that Marcion rejects the Apocalypse of John, nevertheless states that the list of bishops reckoned up to the very beginning affirms John to be its author. He readily uses the testimonies of this book in serious matters and when reasoning against heretics.

### Paragraph 9

The same things are also recorded concerning the blessed Martyr Saint Cyprian,[note 1.16] under the name of John the Apostle in these letters, treatises, and sermons. Eusebius also in chapter 18 of the fifth book of the Ecclesiastical History,[note 1.17] shows that Apollonius, a very ancient writer, uses the testimonies of the Apocalypse of Saint John.[note 1.18] And likewise Theophilus, Bishop of Antioch, who affirms this in chapter 23 of the fourth book of the Ecclesiastical History. Also Origen,[note 1.19], a great man in the church of God, in chapter 25 of the sixth book of the same Eusebius. And he wrote, he says, the Apocalypse, which rested upon the Lord’s breast. &c.

### Paragraph 10

I have hitherto recited the opinions of the most ancient Martyrs and Doctors of the Christian church, concerning the Apocalypse, meaning Justin, Irenaeus, Tertullian, Cyprian, Apollonius, Theophilus, and Origen. I will shortly bring yet more judgments both of Greek and Latin writers, of most authority in the church, agreeing with the minds of those that we have alleged thereto. However, I will first touch briefly such things as Dionysius of Alexandria left written of the same book in the twenty-fifth Chapter of the seventh book of Eusebius, whom I suppose they have followed, as many after him have spoken against this book. He says that diverse who were his predecessors did utterly reject and reject this book. Neither does he hide the cause why they so did, for that the kingdom of Christ is affirmed therein to be earthly. Whereunto doubtless they referred that precious city, and the rest which under terrestrial kinds, figured spiritual things. Which when we, in the treating thereof, have dissolved, declaring that it does not edify the earthly kingdom of Christ, but a spiritual and celestial, no man I trow, will reject a good and godly book, for because certain abusing the testimony thereof, give unto it a wrong sense.

### Paragraph 11

Heretics have twisted many passages of scripture to defend their errors; therefore, should the authority of scripture itself be called into question? I favor neither the Chiliasts nor the Millenaries in regard to this book; I give them no support.

### Paragraph 12

Eusebius says very well toward the end of the third book, speaking of Papias, the first author of the Millenaries. He thought, says he, that after the resurrection Christ should reign here corporally with his thousand years on earth. Which I suppose he thought because he did not understand well the Apostles' words, nor did he consider well those things that were spoken of him under figures, for that he was endowed with a small judgment.

### Paragraph 13

But in the meantime, Dionysius himself, I say, dare not reject this book. He adds immediately that he thinks it not yet to be the book of John the Apostle, but of some other, but who that should be, he knew not. He gathers also by certain conjectures, by the phrase of speech, and handling of the book, and by the unlikeness of wit, that this book should be another man's than he that wrote the Gospel and Epistle. But seeing that the arguments of the story and Epistle are so diverse, that neither are like, and the argument of the book of Revelation most diverse of all: why should it seem marvelous, though it agrees not with them in all things?

### Paragraph 14

No one can deny that there is great agreement in doctrine. The Epistle to the Hebrews appeared to many to support the Novatians or Catharites in chapter six and ten. The difference in style was noted to differ from the rest of Paul’s Epistles. But if we were to judge Scripture in this way, I do not know what could be firm and sure enough. Leaving this disputation in suspense, I will now proceed to present the judgments of other ancient writers concerning this book.

### Paragraph 15

Eusebius, nicknamed Pamphilus, Bishop of Caesarea, living in the time of Emperor Constantine, and a diligent reader of ancient writers, whom many suspect of diminishing the authority of this book to favor their own views, eloquently affirms in the eightieth chapter of the third book of his history that John, exiled to Patmos, wrote his Revelation there, while taunting the tyranny of Domitian.

### Paragraph 16

And where other historians also do the same. He again in chapter 24 of the third book, concerning the Apocalypse, says that the opinions of people are diverse, some approving and others reproving it. Again, when he presents his opinion regarding the canon of the New Testament in chapter 25, he includes the Apocalypse with the books of unquestioned authority, although he does not dissemble that he will show elsewhere what other people think of it. While he does this, he incorporates many more and better, who judged the Apocalypse to be of Saint John the Apostle, and embraced it as a most godly book, than those who denied or reproved it.

### Paragraph 17

Epiphanius, Bishop of Salamis in Cyprus, a Greek author, also clearly ascribes this book to Saint John the Apostle. He has written against heretics in the fifteenth heresy, and Saint Jerome attributes much to this Epiphanius. Saint Jerome himself ascribes this book to John the Apostle, to Paulinus: “The Apocalypse of Saint John,” he says, “has so many sacraments as it has words.” Furthermore, Philastrius, Bishop of Griria, whom Saint Augustine says he saw with Saint Ambrose at Milan, considers those who reject the Apocalypse of John to be heretics, claiming it is not from John the Apostle but from Cerinthus, an heretic. Indeed, Saint Ambrose himself in his writings offers testimonies from the Apocalypse, under the name of Saint John the Apostle.

### Paragraph 18

Augustine embraced this book as Apostolic and referred the same to his church, leaving certain treatises upon the same. Primasius, also Bishop of Utica in Africa, explained it as Apostolic. Of Bed and the residue of that sort, I speak nothing, since his opinion is known to all men. Andreas, also Bishop of Caesarea, wrote upon this book, as Arethas reports in his commentaries, an opinion I declared before.

### Paragraph 19

I believe I have sufficiently demonstrated the authority of this book, against those who diminish it. But the strongest evidence seems to be the book itself, and the way it is handled, proving that it proceeded from the Apostle. We will prove this in the treatise itself. But if those blessed fathers in their time did interpret the Apocalypse for their churches, why should it not be lawful for us also in our time to interpret it for our people, who live at the end of the world, where all things are now more fully realized than they were in their time? Indeed, these things serve most chiefly for us and for our time, especially as we endure and are tested under Antichrist.

### Paragraph 20

Therefore, it is in vain for many to claim that this book is obscure and cannot be understood, and for that very reason should not be read in the church without any profit or benefit.

### Paragraph 21

To say nothing of this, nothing is presented in holy scripture that does not have an excellent fruit. Nor must we immediately despair of true understanding, even though at first sight the holy scripture may seem obscure, which is opened by God himself and is obtained through prayer and godly exercises. Certainly, we are not ignorant that many would prefer that nothing be spoken of the Antichrist, so that he might reign more carelessly and they themselves be less subject to perils. But Christ commands us to trouble him. Let us therefore proceed in the work of the Lord.

### Paragraph 22

And where it offends them that John makes little mention or none of Christ, whereas the manner of the Apostles is always to intimate Christ and the grace of redemption, we suppose this same book will be looked upon more thoroughly to prove the contrary. I will now recite that argument.

### Paragraph 23

The prophet Zechariah in the third chapter [note 1.32] presents the entire mystery of Christ before all people’s eyes in a most evident figure. For he sees Jesus the high priest, clothed in shameful garments, and like a coal taken from the fire, suffering much contradiction from the Devil. Immediately afterward, he sees the same person cast off the shameful clothing and put on white garments, to be glorified, and proclaimed king and priest and savior of all.

### Paragraph 24

The Apostle and Evangelist John explains this figure. First, after the Gospel is presented, he describes Christ in humble clothing, showing how much opposition he endured from the wicked until he was finally nailed to the cross. He also touches on his glory there. However, the Apocalypse appended to it makes this even clearer, showing him in a white garment and in glory, how, after this humbling, he was exalted and obtained a name above all names. And now, being in glory, he nevertheless works in the church, the savior of all the faithful within the church. In his Epistle, he praises this entire mystery of piety and urges it upon all people.

### Paragraph 25

For the whole book is divided into six parts. The first part doth treat of the state of the Church before the coming of our Lord Jesus Christ. The second part doth shew the state of the Church from the time that our Lord Jesus Christ came in the flesh until his ascension into heaven. The third part doth declare the state of the Church from the time that our Lord Jesus Christ ascended into heaven until the last day. The fourth part doth speak of the time when the kingdom of our Lord Jesus Christ shall be set up upon earth. The fifth part doth shew the state of things in heaven, and the sixth part doth describe the last judgment, and the eternal glory of the saints. And as Hierome, Augustine, Irenaeus, and Dionysius have taught, so also doth this division agree with the order of the Apocalypse. For it is not to be understood of six several ages, but of six parts of time, wherein God doth shew his will and purpose, according to his good pleasure. And in every dispensation, he doth work and govern all things, according to his most wise and holy will. And though some men do judge that the Apocalypse is a prophecy of the Antichrist, and of the things that shall come to pass in the end of the world, yet it is not so. For it doth not speak of one particular time, but of all times, and of all the events that shall happen until the last day. And therefore, it is not to be understood of a certain number of years, but of the order and manner of God’s working, and of the things that shall be done in every age, according to his will. And as much as concerneth the authority of the Scripture, it is to be received and understood according to the rule of the Church, and the consent of the Fathers. And in Zurich, we do follow this rule, and do teach the people according to the word of God.

### Paragraph 26

For first is set the title with the beginning and some of the work, and with a brief narration: And all this in the first part of the first chapter.

### Paragraph 27

Secondly, from the midst of the first chapter to the fourth chapter, Christ’s reigning in glory is described, at the right hand of the Father, and it is declared how he is active in the Church by his Spirit and ministry of his word. What things he teaches from heaven, and what the sincere doctrine of the church is: what also is the restoring of churches that have fallen, and the preservation of them.

### Paragraph 28

3 Then from the fourth Chapter to the twelfth, Christ still diligently admonishes his Church through seven seals and seven trumpets, describing the events that will befall the Church, all of which are most justly governed by God himself through the Lamb, Christ.

### Paragraph 29

4 Moreover, from chapter twelve to fifteen, the conflict of the Church with the ancient Serpent and with the old and new beast is more fully described. Here, too, that monstrous tyranny, both old and new, and the very Antichrist himself, are clearly depicted in their colors; nevertheless, these things are afterward also declared again more plainly.

### Paragraph 30

5 And from the fifteenth Chapter to the twenty-second Chapter, are recited the pains and torments of Antichrist, and Antichristians, and the destruction of the same and the condemnation of all the wicked. Also the Judge, Christ, is set forth, and the process of an external judgment is figured. There is also rehearsed the triumph, joy, and reward of Saints. Where also heaven itself is opened to be seen by our eyes, that now we may by faith look into the same. The depth of Hell is opened, that we may look into it also: and may take good heed that we be not thrown thither headlong.

### Paragraph 31

Finally, at the end of chapter twenty-two, follows the conclusion and commendation of the work, with its sealing.

### Paragraph 32

And I will not hide another division of this work, not to be scorned, which I see the commentators have followed. For first, they rehearse the title and beginning. Afterward, they annex the entire work itself, divided into seven visions. Indeed, the number seven is most frequent, and as it were, peculiar to this book. Finally, they add to the conclusion of the work, in a manner comprised in the last chapter. And these visions are contained within their limits.

### Paragraph 33

In the first three chapters, the initial vision is explained, exhibiting Christ to us reigning in glory, governing, ordering, correcting, and preserving his church.

### Paragraph 34

The second vision begins in the fourth chapter and extends to the eighth. That passage depicts God himself and his Christ, presenting them for consideration, and commends their just governance of all things in the world, opening seven seals.

### Paragraph 35

The third vision has seven angels sounding with seven trumpets. This section extends to chapter twelve.

### Paragraph 36

The fourth vision depicts the struggle of the woman with the serpent, and presents to us the old seven-headed beast and the new two-horned beast, which are to be understood as the description of Antichrist, and this is found in chapters twelve, thirteen, and fourteen.

### Paragraph 37

In the fifth vision, seven angels pour out seven vials of God’s wrath, up to chapter seventeen.

### Paragraph 38

From there begins the sixth vision, and it extends to chapter twenty-one, discussing God’s just judgment against Babylon, the great prostitute, and against those who oppose Christ, ultimately against all unrepentant and wicked people.

### Paragraph 39

The seventh and final vision presents to the eyes of all the faithful the glory and eternal blessedness of the Saints. Indeed, this section of the work possesses great grace and affinity with the rest of the things which in this book are all, in a manner, treated by the seventh number. Let the reader follow whichever he prefers.

### Paragraph 40

Now concerning these things, every person may perceive that this book is entirely apostolic and exceedingly profitable to us all, especially those whom the end of the world has overtaken. And this book will be easier to understand now that so many things are, in a manner, fulfilled.

### Paragraph 41

Daniel was thought to have told of stark dreams when before the Monarchies, he prophesied the Monarchies. But after those things were accomplished which he prophesied, he seemed unto many to have compiled a history. The self same, I am sure, thou will judge also: this same book of Saint John. A few prophecies only we shall recite.

### Paragraph 42

In this book, we have a most complete description of Christ reigning in glory, our king, I say, and how he governs the Church and is the Savior of all the faithful. We also have a most excellent description of Christ’s Church, and how it may be built up, repaired, and maintained. Then we have a perfect description of Antichrist, of his followers, and the synagogue of his counsels, crafty schemes, kingdom, cruelty, and destruction. From this, it bids us beware. Moreover, we have a summary of history from Christ’s time until the end of the world.

### Paragraph 43

Finally, a certain and absolute prophecy of things to come, so that we need not the prophecies of Methodius, Cyril, Merlin, Bridget, Nolhard, and certain triflers.

### Paragraph 44

Furthermore, we have great consolation and comfort for the church in adversity, while we see the Lamb open the Seals, and that all things are accomplished by God’s providence, and that there is an end to evil. And the church shall endure forever, despite all the devils in hell. Lastly, we have a most plentiful and sure doctrine of the Judge and final judgment, of punishments and of rewards.

### Paragraph 45

All these things I say, the treatise itself will plainly demonstrate, for our edification through Jesus Christ our Lord.

## OF THE TITLE OF THE whole work, and exposition thereof. The second Sermon. ☞ I said the whole book was contained in six parts.Three members of the first part. Now must we look on the first part: Which hath chiefly three members: The title, beginning, and brief narration. For this present we will only speak of the Title, which is thus.

### Paragraph 46

The revelation of Jesus Christ, which God gave to him, to show to his servants what must shortly come to pass. And he sent and showed by his angel to his servant John, who bore witness to the word of God, and to the testimony of Jesus Christ, and to all things that he saw. Blessed is he who reads, and those who hear the words of the prophecy, and keep those things written therein. For the time is at hand.

### Paragraph 47

This title is abundant and proclaims all beneficial matters that ought to be stated at the beginnings of books.

### Paragraph 48

First, the title, or inscription, of the entire work is set forth: the Apocalypse, or revelation of Jesus Christ, which truly was opened or revealed by Jesus Christ himself.

### Paragraph 49

This title immediately demonstrates that this work is not a human invention, but a divine doctrine: as it was revealed by our Lord, king, and priest Jesus Christ, out of heaven, from the right hand of the Father, exercising there the office of the high priest, and still teaching us profitable things. And although it is also called the Revelation of John, it is attributed to him for no other reason than that, as a scribe, he wrote and presented it.

### Paragraph 50

Again it is yet more plainly declared from whence this Revelation comes: Even from God himself. For he says, which God, namely the Father, gave unto him, to wit, to Christ. For in the holy and blessed Trinity, there is a distinction of persons. And although all things of the Father are also the Son's, and all things of the Son are likewise the Father's, yet Scripture mentions the Father giving to the Son, and the Son receiving from the Father.

### Paragraph 51

The ancient writers have consistently explained this as occurring through the mystery of dispensation. For the Son received from the Father, as a man—and as the very Son of God says—"Glorify me with the glory that I had with you before the world existed."

### Paragraph 52

Moreover, the Son is the wisdom, word, and mouth of the Father, by whom God in times past and now spoke and speaks to the Fathers, Prophets, Apostles, and to the universal church. The Father, by dispensation, gave to his Son this office, that he should be Bishop. For no man has seen God at any time; the only-begotten Son, who is in the bosom of the Father, has revealed him to us. Let us know, therefore, that this is a divine Revelation, which God the Father, loving mankind, has revealed by the only Bishop Christ to his Church. And so it joins together the Father and the Son, that nevertheless the holy distinction of persons remains safe.

### Paragraph 53

Now also is added, for what purpose God the Father has revealed, or granted the gift of revealing, namely, the office of priesthood to his Son, our Lord Jesus Christ: so that, being revealed, he might demonstrate it, and as it were set it before the eyes of his servants, that is, his worshippers and Christians, who are called the servants of God for their willing obedience. And as a servant owes all that he has or is worth to his lord, so we owe ourselves whole to God, and all that is ours, or else we are free, and not bound.

### Paragraph 54

It is also declared to whom this revelation is opened [?]: to all the servants of God. If, therefore, you desire to be called a servant of God, hear this book and remember it; and know that this book is prepared for you by God.

### Paragraph 55

After he summarizes briefly what Christ revealed to John, things that must shortly come to pass. Therefore, the destinies of the Church are recounted, what good and evil things will happen to the faithful, and likewise what punishments must be inflicted on the wicked.

### Paragraph 56

Let no one understand this passage as implying a requirement, necessity, as though God did not work freely;[note 2.9] or that the wicked commit evil not through their own fault, but by God’s compulsion. Good things must be done, because God, binding himself to us willingly through his promise, cannot fail to do what he promises and declares he will do; nevertheless, he acts freely.

### Paragraph 57

Good things must be done in the godly community, for the nature of grace and faith is such that ungodliness is characterized by contempt and transgression. Therefore, it must also be punished. And because the world is as it is, there must needs be innumerable heresies and calamities. He says these things must shortly be done that are revealed, for certain things began in the very time of Saint John. And although many things are found to be done a thousand years after, Saint Peter says, “A thousand years before the Lord are as it were yesterday.” Therefore, this Revelation pertains to the times of the primitive and last church, and declares whatever things shall happen unto it until the last judgment. Yea, and how it shall reign forever.

### Paragraph 58

Moreover, the manner of revealing is also touched. For Christ revealed those things, sending by his angel, or his angel sent forth, to whom he gave commandment what he should say and do. Whereupon this angel is afterward also called Christ, because he represented the person of Christ. Therefore, the angel in this book, but Christ always, must be considered, the true Author of all these things. Indeed, the divinity of Christ is here commended to us, when we hear that Christ is the Lord of angels. Paul reasoned more at large about this to the Hebrews. Moses in the twelfth chapter of Numbers sets forth chiefly three manners of prophecy or revelation. First, by vision, of which sort many are ascribed to Daniel, one notable to Peter in the tenth chapter of Acts, and likewise to Paul. And into this form falls also the Apocalypse. Secondly, by dream: of which sort were those of Pharaoh and Nebuchadnezzar, kings, of whom Joseph and Daniel were interpreters. The Prophet Joel in the second chapter mentions visions and dreams. For in the New Testament also there are very many holy and prophetical dreams. Lastly, Moses sets forth a skillful exposition, as many were made to Moses and to the Apostles. Into whose fellowship the Apocalypse comes after a sort also, where visions are openly explained. Here appears the unspeakable goodness of God, which in many ways procures and works our salvation, and pleasantly prepared offers it to us to enjoy. Unhappy is he who does not know these things.

### Paragraph 59

Besides this, much mention is made unto whom Christ has opened this divine and most excellent revelation, even to John. He commends him, that is himself (for so it was expedient for the confuting of his adversaries, seeing that Paul also many times maintained his authority against the fallen Apostles) by four epithets. For first he calls himself the servant of Christ. This is the eldest and noblest title, which the fathers, Prophets, and Apostles have used. For they dedicate and consecrate to God. Secondly, John testified the word of God amongst the Apostles, most expressly declaring the divinity of Christ, especially where he testified and said, “In the beginning was the word.” Moreover, he is a witness of the witness of Jesus Christ: under which name the Lord himself in the Gospel, and John in the twelfth chapter of his Gospel, comprised the whole evangelical doctrine. And was a seeing witness of all these things. For in the first chapter we have seen his glory, he says, and in the nineteenth chapter he saw water and blood gush out of the Lord’s side. In his epistle, he says, “We have seen and have heard.”

### Paragraph 60

Arethas notes that in certain Greek copies is added what is also found in the Greek copy of Spain. And whatever he has heard, and whatever is, and whatever must be done after this.

### Paragraph 61

That same John is the author of this book,[note 2.18] who, as he saw the Lord in the flesh upon earth, so he saw him in spirit, revealing these things in heaven, and who presents to the church sights most certain and sure. This John was that beloved disciple of the Lord, who at the last supper rested upon his breast, to whom in his last will he bequeathed his mother on the cross, one virgin to another.

### Paragraph 62

He alone stood by at the altar of the cross when Christ died: a witness to the true death and to our purification. He lived until the time of Emperor Trajan, as Eusebius recounts in his chronicles, citing Irenaeus, who notes the year as one hundred and three from the birth of Christ. Dorotheus, a very ancient writer, affirms that John lived sixty years.

### Paragraph 63

Lastly, the benefit of this godly work or revelation has been touched upon, so that readers and hearers might be spurred to diligence. For this book is also called a prophecy. This is because it foretells future events, and is thus prophecy in the New Testament. Moreover, a prophecy is an exposition that opens and explains the Old Prophets. It promises blessedness to those who read, hear, and keep the things written in this book. Blessedness encompasses the benefits of the present life, insofar as the Lord allows them to the faithful, but chiefly concerns the life to come. If the benefit of this book has been spoken of before in the first sermon. And note that it is not enough to simply read or hear this book; it must be put into practice and kept diligently.

### Paragraph 64

For the Lord also said in the Gospel: Blessed are those who hear the word of God and keep it. Those therefore who shape their life according to this book are blessed. For both they avoid the deception of Antichrist, remain in the faith of Christ, and live forevermore. &c.

### Paragraph 65

And he concludes the title with an exclamation, by which he greatly stirs up the listeners: For the time is at hand, as though he should say: Let no one think here that strange things, and those which do not concern him, are told here, which will come to pass at length after many ages; they belong to every one of us. For they are written of matters that chiefly concern us, and even of our own affairs. Thus he shows that this book is profitable for worlds, men, and ages. God the Father, by his Son, teaches profitable things, and admonishing in due season, be praised world without end. Amen.

## Sermon 3: OF THE BEGINNING OF THIS book, and the Apostles salutation: wherein are declared the mysteries chiefly of Christ, ſecondly of our whole faith and redemption.

### Paragraph 66

John to the seven congregations in Asia. Grace be with you and peace from him who is, and who was, and who is to come, and from the seven spirits which are sent before his throne. And from Jesus Christ, the faithful witness, the firstborn of the dead, and Lord over the kings of the earth. To him who loved us and washed us from our sins in his own blood, and made us kings and priests to God his Father, to him be glory and dominion forevermore. Amen. Behold, he comes with clouds, and every eye shall see him, and they also who pierced him. And all kindreds of the earth shall wail over him. Even so. Amen. I am Alpha and Omega, the beginning and the ending, says the Lord Almighty, who is, and who was, and who is to come.

### Paragraph 67

Another section of the first part of this book contains a beginning or preface, wherein is the Apostle’s salutation, whereby he first describes the whole mystery of Christ, and secondly of our faith and redemption. For so the Apostles were accustomed in the beginning of their writings to comprise a brief summary of salutation. This practice is evident everywhere in Paul’s Epistles. By this description, he gains the goodwill and attention of all people.

### Paragraph 68

The apostles’ salutation or greeting is nothing else, but a blessing. Blessing is an old, customary practice by which the patriarchs wished God’s good things—both of body and soul—upon their children. This is described at length in Genesis. Also, the high priest had been commanded to bless the people, as we read in Numbers 6, especially that he should put God’s name upon the people. Therefore, it is a superstition to say that God, from whom every good gift descends from above, blesses, that is, gives good things, but that ministers or men only wish. And the Lord in the law promises that he will grant those things to the people which the high priests should wish them. Therefore, neither words nor shaven crowns, but the truth and power of God give the gifts. We ought not therefore to doubt that God will grant to us also the apostolic blessing, that being reconciled and accepted of God we might have peace. And first, Saint John repeats his name, lest we should doubt of the authority who we see Christ to have used as scribe and interpreter to all congregations. But he does not repeat that he himself is that servant of God, and witness or apostle of Jesus Christ. It sufficed to have heard that at the first beginning. Therefore, he teaches modesty and humility also, which have obtained great gifts. Afterward, he signifies to whom he writes, and to whom this book pertains, to the seven churches of Asia, the names of which he will utter shortly after. And Arethas, bishop of Caesarea, by the seven churches, says he, and by the seven numbers, signified the multitude of churches that are in all places. So also Primasius, bishop of Utica in Africa, explains the seven numbers. Therefore, this salutation, this book, and the whole doctrine of Jesus Christ, written by Saint John, pertains to the whole universal church of Christ throughout all the world, and in all times and ages. Whereupon it belongs to all of us as many as are of us in the church of Christ. For although the epistles are entitled to the Romans and Galatians, it does not follow that they are not ours. And he writes expressly to the churches of Asia, not to the churches of Jerusalem or Judaea, that he might show that the kingdom of Christ is come already to the Gentiles. And as God from the beginning chose Israel, in whom he might set forth a perfect example of the church and commonwealth, so from the beginning of the new Testament, he chose those seven churches of Asia, which he might set forth to the whole Christian world. But if Rome had been set in the first place among the churches, as Ephesus is, good God, how much would the Roman sort make of it, for the establishing of their supremacy?

### Paragraph 69

And the manner of the Apostles’ salutations wishes grace. Grace is the favor of God, and the reconciliation meant, whereby God the Father for Christ’s sake is made one with us, our sins pardoned, and we adopted for his children. Thereof arises the peace and tranquility of mind and the desire of concord with all men.

### Paragraph 70

And he shows abundantly who grants the church his blessing, that is to say, grace, reconciliation, and peace—God, and God three in persons, the Father, the Son, and the Holy Ghost, one God in essence. But here he discerns the persons very well. From him that is, to wit, the Father; and from the seven spirits, that is, from the Holy Ghost; and from Jesus Christ—this is the diversity of persons. And the signification of the unity is, when after the properties of persons are declared, he adds, “I am Alpha and Omega.” &c. And that the Holy Ghost is set here in the midst, it disorders not the mystery of the Trinity, but appears to be an argument that he is the spirit as well of the Father as of the Son, and that he proceeds from both. As it is also proved by the words of our Lord, the xiv, xv, and xvi chapters of John. Here is also described the whole wholesome mystery first of Christ, then of the catholic faith, and of our redemption, so that herein you may find the chiefest articles of the Apostles’ Creed, and have here a most goodly description of Christ our Lord. Hereof all men shall judge how truly some say that this book, contrary to the common opinion of the Apostles, makes little mention of Christ and of faith.

### Paragraph 71

The father, as source and origin, from whom the Son is generated, is first described: for it is he who is, who was, and who is to come. John took those words from Moses in the third and thirty-fourth chapter of Exodus, and from many testimonies of Isaiah. And he says nothing but that God the Father is an eternal essence, which exists by and of itself, and gives life to all, and in all preserves it. And that this essence is such that it has always existed without beginning. For this is what he joins to being, or existence; he was. He adds, and he who is to come, which shall be, and shall remain even to the end, and to everlastingness without end. The Greeks derive [illegible] from running, for that concerning and running, he meddles with all matters; is everywhere present, bringing help to the godly, or restraining and punishing the wicked.

### Paragraph 72

And the Holy Spirit, where he is but one,[note 3.8] for the sevenfold—that is, all manner of grace and manifold gifts—is here called, as I may say, Septenary or of the seventh number. And from the seven spirits, John says that it is from that Spirit, which is endowed with sevenfold grace. Those diverse gifts are, in a way, explained in Essay in the eleventh chapter and elsewhere in the scriptures. He is said to be in the sight of the throne, that is, before the throne of God, joined truly in governance with the Father and the Son. For the throne is often used to represent the kingdom. Therefore, the Holy Spirit is of the same glory, power, and majesty as God.

### Paragraph 73

Now he has come to Christ, whom by his attributes he describes most abundantly. You know that Jesus is the proper name of Christ, which Matthew explains: a Savior. Christ is the surname of his office and dignity, as if you were to say, “anointed,” that is, bishop and king.

### Paragraph 74

First he calls Christ our Lord a faithful witness, as in chapters 49 and 50 of Isaiah. For he was sent by the Father to the world from heaven, an Apostle, who should testify the will of God, what he would have done with men. Namely, that he would save the world by his Son, and by faith in him, which is obedient to the law of God. For he must do the will of his Father. This Christ is a faithful witness, that is sure, constant, and true: of whose doctrine no man ought to doubt. No man has seen God at any time; the only-begotten Son, who is in the Father’s bosom, has revealed him. This, therefore, is the doctrine of the church. Whoever dissent from him are to be eschewed. Hear him, says the Father.

### Paragraph 75

He is the firstborn of the dead. For he truly died for our sins, and rose again from the dead, and was made the firstborn of the dead, Lord and conqueror of death; in him we see that we also shall rise again, and in what manner. As it is written in 1 Corinthians 15. And just as in the first part he shadows the humanity of Christ, wherein he also taught his deity, in that he was the faithful, true, and catholic bishop, and is yet at this day; so in the second, the articles of our belief concerning the death of Christ and his resurrection are confirmed. To these may also be added the article of the resurrection of the dead.

### Paragraph 76

3 Christ is prince over the kings of the earth, truly a monarch, and Lord of all rulers: He has taken a name above all names, the Lord of angels and of all creatures, to whom all things are subject, as the Apostle expounds. Colossians and Philippians 2. And he does not abolish laws and magistrates who will be king of kings and Lord of lords. For if there were no kings, how could Christ be king of kings? The most sacred Emperors, Constantine, Theodosius, and Justinian, knew themselves to be clients of Christ: their kingdom was Christ's, and they were to be subjects. Christ acknowledges these as his, by whom he governs those he has redeemed with his blood. Those who proudly rule over the people, boast themselves to be lords of all things, and do not acknowledge Christ to be monarch over all, are stark mad. And here are comprehended such things as we confess in the articles of our faith, that Christ ascended into heaven and sits on the right hand of the Father: that is, that he has received the highest power over all things in heaven and earth. Ephesians 1. Acts 2.

### Paragraph 77

Christ has loved us with incomparable love. For he himself says: “Greater love has no one than that someone should lay down his life for his friends.” The Apostle amplifies this in the fifth chapter to the Romans. It was exceedingly great love that moved Christ to come down from heaven and be incarnate and to redeem us by his death. With a free love he loved us, provoked by no merit of ours. For as John in his canonical Epistle speaks the same of the Father: “In this is love, not that we have loved God, but that he has loved us and sent his son as an atoning sacrifice for our sins,” so it is to be understood of the Son, that he has and does bear us great good will, not moved thereto through our love, with which we have embraced him. And of the free love toward mankind, he gave himself unto death and washed us from our sins. For it is straightway added, by his blood.

Where three things seem to be observed concerning us. First, that Christ washes, purges, purifies, or cleanses the faithful: and that most fully, not partly. He alludes to the washings of the law, which he also expounded. For David says: “Purge me with hyssop, and I shall be made clean; wash me, and I shall be whiter than snow.” The same phrase of speech is repeated by Essay in the first chapter. Micah also says: “The Lord will return and will have mercy on us; he will trample underfoot our iniquities. And you will throw into the depths of the sea all their sins.” And the Lord says: “I will pour clean water upon you, and you will be cleansed from all your filthiness.” The Lord Christ accomplishing these things, washes us, purges, and cleanses thoroughly, as well from the fault as the pain. He cleanses us from our sins, not from one, but from all. This which thing is proved both by former testimonies, and again in the first and second Epistle of John. Lastly, the manner of purifying is set forth, by blood. For without the shedding of blood, no remission was made. Therefore through the mediation of death and bloodshedding, full remission of all sins was obtained for the faithful.

They who bring forth any other manner of forgiveness of sins, are injurious to the death and blood of the son of God. And here we may plainly see set forth an article of the Apostolic creed: I believe the forgiveness of sins.

### Paragraph 78

In the fifth place, the effect of our redemption and purification is shown. For Christ has brought it to pass that as many of us as believe in the Father through the Son of God should be kings and priests to God and to his Father. Arethas and the copy of Complutum read not “kings” but “kingdom,” which is not read amiss. For we are the kingdom of God, because God by his Spirit, not the flesh nor the world, ought to reign in us. And when we submit to the Spirit’s governance, we are the kingdom of God. Paul discusses this at length in the sixth chapter to the Romans. Moreover, we are made kings—that is, free—by Christ, so that we should not serve the devil, the flesh, and the world, according to that saying of Zechariah, being delivered from the hands of our enemies, that we might serve him without fear in holiness and righteousness before him all the days of our life. And Christ has consecrated priests with his Spirit and blood, that we should offer up to God spiritual sacrifices, ourselves pure, prayers and praises, and alms. For Peter and Paul testify that these are spiritual oblations. John took these things from Exodus: For we of the Gentiles who have believed have succeeded in the place of the people of Israel, rejected Christ through unbelief. And these things give light to that article of the Creed, “I believe in the holy catholic church, the communion of saints.” For we, as many of us as believe, are the fellowship of God’s people, sanctified through Christ to the service of God. Of whom are these things said?

### Paragraph 79

In the sixth place, in the description of Christ, he shows that the glory and rule are due to God alone through Christ, for the church forevermore. We give glory to God when we ascribe our salvation and all goodness to his goodness, not to our own strength and merits. We give him rule when we acknowledge him to be Lord and head in the church, working by himself, not by the saints in heaven, to whom he has granted power. Not by the Pope, whom he has not constituted Vicar on earth. The whole glory and rule is Christ’s.

### Paragraph 80

Seventhly in the description follows the coming of Christ unto judgment, and the manner of his coming. As a cloud took him up from the eyes of the Apostles, so shall he come in clouds to judge the living and the dead. The scripture witnesses. And he adds that the eyes of all men shall see the judge, even of those who have pleased him. Whereof we gather two things, first, that the judgment shall be universal, wherein men arising shall see Christ with their own eyes. Another thing, that Christ shall come to judge in the same flesh in which he was wounded and pierced, hung upon the cross, was buried and rose again. This place is taken out of Zachariah, and is cited also in Saint John’s Gospel. And it behooves that his body be showed to the whole world full of prints and marks, that hereof may be judged the godly and also the ungodly: they that then have believed in such a redeemer, these that then have rejected and contemned such a one. Of these we understand that is added: And they shall wail, for that in deed they have neglected their own salvation, which the wise man discourses at large. Moreover, lest any should doubt of those things that are spoken of the judgment, and of the lamentation of the wicked (as Saint Peter said, the contemners and mockers of the judgment should be), he adds a kind of confirmation, even so. Amen.

### Paragraph 81

In them also is explained the article of the creed of Christ who will judge the living and the dead. He concludes this passage with these words: “I am Alpha and Omega,” with “that which follows (the beginning and end)” omitted in some copies. It seems that interpretation of that same, “I am Alpha and Omega,” crept in from the margin. It is a saying of Saint John the Apostle, “I am Alpha and Omega.” Heretics, such as Basilides and Valentine, were greatly delighted in letters. But against those lettered heretics, John speaks plainly by the mouth of Christ: “I am Alpha and Omega.” If anything ought to be ascribed to letters, I am all this whole everlasting virtue, essence, and eternity. For the sense is, that God is the beginning and end, that is, eternal, unspeakable, best, and greatest. Those things are repeated: “He that is, which was,” and so forth, which were explained before. There is added, “almighty.” For hereby is declared the unity and majesty of God, of whom the Trinity was opened also before. Hereby also the authority of this book is confirmed, the author of which is shown to be that God eternal and almighty. To whom be glory.

## Sermon 4: ¶ Of the Narration of this book, where also is discoursed of the place and time, and of the author of this Revelation.

### Paragraph 82

I, John, your brother and companion in tribulation, and in the kingdom and patience which are in Jesus Christ, was on the island of Patmos for the word of God and for the testimony of Jesus Christ. I was in the spirit on the Lord’s Day and heard behind me a great voice, as if of a trumpet, saying, “I am Alpha and Omega, the first and the last.” And I saw another vision, and it was commanded that I write in a book and send it to the congregations which are in Asia, to Ephesus, and to Smyrna, and to Pergamum, and to Thyatira, and to Sardis, and to Philadelphia, and to Laodicea.

### Paragraph 83

The last section of the first book shows us a brief account wherein the Apostle John declares the time and place of this Revelation, and by whose authority he wrote and sent it to the seven churches in Asia.

### Paragraph 84

And again now, for the third time, the name of John is repeated. He saw undoubtedly that there would be some who, to the end they might take away the use and benefit of this book, would question the author. Against whom he repeats and reiterates his name so often, lest we should doubt and lose the great value of so worthy a book.

### Paragraph 85

He adds certain things to his name, which instruct concerning the state of the Apostle, and certain profitable matters. First, he calls himself a brother, namely of those churches, and of all ours. As where I have admonished you that in the seventh number are comprised all churches of all times throughout the whole world. We are all, so many believe, the children of one heavenly father. And therefore all spiritual brethren in Christ, coheirs with Christ, and heirs of God. Which thing Paul taught after Christ.

And seeing our dignity is so great, let us once be ashamed of our misdeeds, lest our memory be put out of this most noble and celestial family. It is a shame that a brother of Christ, of John and all the Apostles should degenerate. And so on.

But why have they not so instantly urged this brotherhood, as the Monks have promoted their forged fraternities, the Rosaries of the Virgin Mary and of Saints? Because that was free, and cost nothing. But the Monks sell theirs dear. They are therefore deceivers and seducers.

### Paragraph 86

After he calls himself a partaker in affliction, or oppression and persecution, as he who was even now banished by the Emperor Domitian and lived in exile. And he joins together and does not separate himself in the evil: that is common to all the faithful brethren. And truly it is one and the same persecution that vexed the Apostles and torments us at this day. Let us therefore rejoice that we have the Apostles and all the Martyrs of Christ as fellows in our trouble and affliction, that we are broken and bruised with the heavy burden of evils. Let us therefore be patient and long-suffering. For it is not enough to be afflicted and vexed with every kind of evil (for many without any fruit or praise at all endure most grievous pains). But it becomes us also to be patient in adversity. Therefore John at this present joins with all, patience. For the Lord said in the Gospel, “In your patience you shall possess your souls.”

### Paragraph 87

After he adds unto tribulation and patience a kingdom, and that an heavenly not a terrestrial kingdom.[note 4.5] And he brings in the kingdom for the comfort of the patient people. For also the Apostle Paul said, a certain and sure saying. For if we die with Christ, we shall live also with him: If we suffer, we shall reign with him. &c. Let us always here with comfort our selves in adversity. For we are brought low, that we might once be exalted again, 2 Corinthians 4. And all these things are concluded in Christ Jesus, by whom we are both the children and brethren of God, and suffer many things patiently, and are made partakers of his kingdom. For even for these things must we thank him and his mercies, and not our own merit.

### Paragraph 88

Let us here note also what and how great has been the humility of the greatest and worthy Apostle of God, considering his state: Not pleasant, but hard, yet in the patience of Christ joyful. But where are they now, those who glory in the name of Apostles? Who, meanwhile, swelling with pride, are addicted to filthy pleasures? I warn you, we must flee from them as from apostates.

### Paragraph 89

And now he shows the place where this divine revelation was made to him, where also he was commanded by God to write the same. The place was the Isle of Patmos. This island is accounted among the islands called Sporades by Pliny in the fourth book and twelfth chapter. It lay opposite Asia and the city of Ephesus, and was in the sight both of Europe and Africa, so that it seemed to be as it were a middle seat, or holy chair, out of which Christ preached through John from heaven to the whole world. And indeed the councils of God are wonderful, and his goodness is unspeakable, which reveals so great mysteries, as it were in the Roman prison or Babylonian captivity, to his faithful.

### Paragraph 90

Neither does he hide the cause of his coming into the island, saying, “I was there for the word of God and the testimony of Jesus Christ.” The word of God is the very voice of God, called by John with a unique expression the word or sermon of God, as appears in the first chapter of John. The testimony of Jesus Christ is the Gospel itself, which Jesus testified and which his disciples testified concerning Jesus. Therefore, for the confession and preaching of Jesus Christ and his wholesome Gospel (for so he explained how he became a partaker of affliction), John was apprehended in Asia and led by soldiers to Rome, that he might plead his cause before Emperor Domitian, who, by his cruel nature, condemned the innocent. And he was put into a cauldron of hot boiling oil. When he escaped unharmed from that, he was carried to Patmos. He answered no other matter before the emperor than Paul did twenty-seven years before, under Nero. This occurred in the fourteenth or fifteenth year of Domitian and twenty-four years after the destruction of the city of Jerusalem and sixty-six years after the birth of our Lord. Domitian, who sought to be called a god, was slain by his own men after many murders and cruel acts, and died himself a shameful death in the fifteenth year of his reign. The authors of this account are Suetonius in the life of Domitian, Tertullian in *The Prescriptions Against Heretics*, Eusebius in his chronicles and in the third book of the ecclesiastical history, chapters seventeen and eighteen. And to this is added the common consent of all writers.

### Paragraph 91

Moreover he notes the time also, [note 4.9], in which these mysteries began to be revealed to him, [illegible], in that solemn day of the Lord, namely Sunday. For so have the ancient fathers called one of the Sabbaths, that is to say the first day of the week, wherein Christ rose again from the dead, as in Matthew xxviii and Mark xvi. And this day have the churches chosen for themselves in place of the Sabbath day, as holy in remembrance of the Lord’s resurrection, wherein they might keep their sacred and solemn assemblies. For that this day was solemnized and consecrated for assemblies in the congregation of Corinth, it appears manifestly in the sixteenth chapter of the first Epistle to the Corinthians, where the Apostle commands to lay apart their collections on one of the Sabbaths. The same day also the faithful did celebrate their service with Paul, in the twentieth chapter of the Acts. Where Sozomenus reports in the eighth chapter of the first book of the *ecclesiastical history*, that great Constantine made certain holy days, and even the Lord’s day, which is called by the Heathen Sunday, it is to be understood that he renewed rather the custom of the Apostles and catholic church, than to have newly instituted the same. And freely of their own accord have the churches received that day; for we read not that it was anywhere commanded. And the congregations saw how it was altogether necessary that there should be a certain time in which the saints should meet and come together. They chose therefore the day of the resurrection, neither did they maliciously contend among themselves for these things, as the histories testify was done in the church afterward. And at this day verily, the superstitious holy days being abrogated, it is better to observe certain and moderate days, and to keep peace and quietness in the church.

### Paragraph 92

But where this Apostle knew that the faithful on that day served God in all assemblies, where he could not be present in body, in spirit and contemplation he was with them. And as he was thus in the spirit and contemplation of divine matters, and in holy prayers, he heard a voice, of which I will speak hereafter. But here we are presently taught what is the religion of the Sunday, and how it is fitting to observe it. Finally, worldly men are rebuked, who pollute and break it with profane works and affairs. David, in his time, suffered persecution from Saul, lamented chiefly that he might not come to the Lord’s tabernacle. Our people, however, count it a great felicity never to enter into the fellowship of the Saints. And to abuse the Sunday, in gaming, drinking, dancing, and worldly business.

### Paragraph 93

These things declared in this manner, he comes now to the Revelation, setting forth before the clear commandment of God, by which he was commanded both to write the things revealed and also to send them to the seven churches of Asia. What chiefly pertains to the manner and majesty of the Revelation is that he heard a voice, and that as the sound of a trumpet. For so we read it was done in giving the law at Mount Sinai. Now it is declared who that voice was, and who the author of the Revelation. Truly, it was the eternal God, who calls himself Alpha and Omega, that is, the beginning and the end; or as it is said in Essay, first and last. Of this elsewhere.

### Paragraph 94

The commandment which has several parts follows. First, the Lord commands John to see and to write the things he saw, that is to say, the Apocalypse. And he should write neither with the sand nor on the wall, but in a book: truly for the edification and profit of the church present, and of all posterity. Afterward, he also commanded to send those writings to seven congregations, and truly to all the churches of the whole world for all times and ages. Therefore, all these things belong to the provision of congregations, and that of all that be, have been, or shall be.

### Paragraph 95

Here we learn how great is the authority of the scriptures. It was not written nor compiled in books, but by God’s commandment. There are notable testimonies of the book of Moses, in the thirty-fourth chapter of Exodus and the thirty-first chapter of Deuteronomy. And to say nothing of the rest of the prophets, was not Jeremiah commanded to write his sermons again, which King Jehoiakim had cut in pieces and burnt? Doubtless Saint Peter bears witness that the prophets received the mysteries of God to no other end than that they should reveal them to us; which indeed could only be done by the scriptures. Now John is most clearly commanded to write. What shall we say, that he is also commanded to send his writings to the congregations? From this we gather again that God wills right well for the congregations, and even for every one of us. Let us beware and take heed that we do not unworthily reject so great benefits of God, to whom be praise and glory.

## Sermon 5: ¶ THE BEGINNING OF THE work is made, and a most goodly description to us exhibited of Christ king and bishop in glory, and nevertheless woorkyng in the Church.

### Paragraph 96

And I turned myself to see the voice that spoke with me. And when I had turned, I saw seven golden lampstands. And in the midst of the seven lampstands, one like the Son of Man, clothed in a linen garment down to the ground, and girded about the chest with a golden girdle. His head and his hair were white, as white wool, and like snow. And his eyes were as a flame of fire, and his feet like brass, as though they had been heated in a furnace, and his voice as the sound of many waters. And he had in his right hand seven stars, and out of his mouth went out a sharp two-edged sword, and his face shone even as the sun in its strength.

### Paragraph 97

Such things as have been treated of hitherto in this book are in place of a prologue or preface, as they term it. Now at last the matter itself will be presented to us. Therefore follows the second part of this book, which extends to the fourth chapter. In this, Christ is described to us with his catholic church. For first indeed is set before us the most sacred image of Christ our Lord, teaching what sort of one he is on the right hand of his Father in glory, and how he, sitting on the right hand of his Father, works nevertheless in his church continually, never absent, present always. Of what sort moreover the church is here on earth, is figured in those seven congregations. Here therefore are shown the excellent gifts of churches, and again shameful errors: How the Lord Christ confirms those who are sliding and ready to fall, establishes those that stand, strengthens the weakhearted, restrains the foolish hardy, and preserves things that are corrupt. Finally, how faithful ministers of the church must work and travel with the people committed to their care. For here is exceedingly taught what is the repairing and preservation of churches. Where also a brief summary of the whole ecclesiastical and some doctrine brought in to an abridgement, shall be set before us. For here is repeated from heaven of Christ in glory, the doctrine of true religion, which he had set forth more plentifully when he was yet here on earth: and here most aptly applies it to churches, after consideration of the same.

### Paragraph 98

And in most goodly order the words are knit together (as likewise the whole book is written with plain words and hanging right well together; they are deceived who think it to be loose pieces or unbound collections). John heard a voice behind him crying. Whereupon he turned backward that he might see the voice speaking, that is to wit, him that spoke. For Arethas also admonishes that there is a trope in the words. For no man sees, but hears the voice. After turning to see, he saw a figure of Christ our Savior. Therefore, when the Lord speaks, let us turn also with all our heart,[note 5.2] that we may likewise deserve to see the mysteries of the kingdom of God, for he gladly reveals himself to such as turn and desire heavenly things. And from those that neglect the mysteries of the kingdom of God, all things of salvation are hidden.

### Paragraph 99

Furthermore, John depicts to us the image of Christ, our universal king and chief bishop, seated in glory.[note 5.3] Within this description are contained the principal matters concerning Christ. For such a glimpse of Christ is here given us as our frail flesh can perceive in this world. But we shall see him ultimately in the world to come, as he is, in the fullness of his majesty, wherein will be joy and everlasting life: but this in this corrupt world is yet granted to no man.

### Paragraph 100

So much therefore is permitted to us who live yet in this world to be seen, as is profitable, and as our weakness may perceive. But this is not small or insignificant; it is great, abundant, and full of spiritual joy, I mean if we behold these mysteries of God with a faithful eye and a mind eager for godly matters. And there is no doubt that these are certain and true things that are revealed to us here. For they are revealed by the very Son of God. Let us not wish to see more, or desire greater things than these are, but take pleasure in those which Christ has granted us. And let us know for certainty that a wonderful benefit of God is given to us in this vision. For who would not desire to see Christ in glory, sitting on the right hand of the Father? Who does not desire to know what our Savior does in heaven? And how, being in heaven, is he nevertheless present with his church on earth? But this sacred and holy image instructs in all these points the faithful of Christ most fully. However, this image of Christ is not to be portrayed with colors, since colors cannot attain to its majesty, but with the ecclesiastical doctrine, which has the promise of the Spirit of Christ, and is therefore more evident and only fitting for its true expression. Let us also print this image, not upon any dead surface with colors that will perish and fade, but in our hearts through the living Spirit of God, which may also keep it in our minds, never to be erased. And the things spoken in the second and third chapters of this book are derived from this description of Christ, that the majesty of the thing might invite us to singular diligence. The matter is very plain.

### Paragraph 101

First we are taught who it is, whose image is to be exhibited to us: not the Son of Man himself in his own substance, but like the Son of Man. The Son of Man, according to the Gospel, is called Christ himself, very God and man. Here he did not show himself to be seen as John in his own substance, but in the form of an angel, that represents Christ. This thing is found more than once in the book. We shall therefore refer all these things unto Christ, not to the angel, which is the minister of Christ in the mystery. And we shall see Christ in his own substance, what time our base body shall flit from hence, and being raised from the dead shall be glorified. In the meantime, the soul from the death of the body till it rises again, shall clearly have the fruition of the sight of Christ. Wherein, as I said before, shall be the chief joy and felicity. We shall now therefore see Christ as it were in a glass, and so much as shall suffice us. The Lord open to us the eyes of our mind.

### Paragraph 102

He tells moreover, where he saw Christ in the midst of seven candlesticks. By and by we shall perceive that the candlesticks must be understood as the churches. Christ is then in the midst of the church. He sits verily on the right hand of the Father, and according to the propriety of his divine body, he is but in one place, and in no more. As Saint Augustine declares abundantly in the 57th Epistle to Dionysius. Yet for as much as he is also very God, he is likewise in the midst of the church, as he promised in the Gospel: Wherever two or three are gathered in my name, I am in the midst of them. And again: Behold, I am with you unto the world’s end. Therefore, by his divine power, Christ remains and works in the present church, and is not absent. (Leave therefore to inquire, what Christ does on the right hand of his Father, whether he sits continually?) And he is verily in the midst of the churches, fixed to a place, but showing himself indifferently to all, as helpful. For he neither accepts persons, nor sleeps. He is not painted, he is not idle, nothing regarding matters of the church: but is chiefly and only attentive to the salvation of the same. Such a one he promised himself to be in the 14th, 15th, and 16th chapters of John. And saying Christ is in the midst of the church, what Vicar moreover shall he have? Shall he have that enemy which is directly against him? For a Vicar is in the stead of one absent: but Christ is in the middle of the church, present, not absent.

### Paragraph 103

In the text following, Christ is described most plentifully, many things are ascribed unto him: and it is declared in what sort Christ is in the midst of the church. And first indeed is showed what garment he hath on: to wit, both priestly and princely. By which thing is figured what manner of one Christ is in heaven and in earth: to wit, bishop and king, intercessor, mediator, and sacrifice, a most perfect sanctification and justification, a redeemer and deliverer of the faithful to his father, evermore working the salvation of his faithful. As Paul teaches, Romans 8; Hebrews 7. A pouch [?] is found amongst the apparel of Aaron, and it is a priestly garment. Whereof Jerome writes to Fabiola, concerning the priestly garment. The second vesture of linen is a coat down to the foot, of double linen: which Josephus calls *Bissina*. And it is called in Hebrew *ketheneth*, in Greek [illegible]. This cleaves just to the body, and is so narrow and straight-sleeved that there is no wrinkle at all in the garment, and came down to the legs. This was verily white and clean. For the Lord Christ is an undefiled priest, Hebrews 7. Neither wears he again a foul vesture, as he did, Zechariah 3. Nor a purple, as in John 19. But a bright one, as he that hath obtained a name above all names. But his girdle or belt is worn of soldiers and triumphant persons: and it signifies in Christ the dignity royal. For Christ is king, deliverer, and redeemer of the faithful. His victory is ours. He hath overcome Satan, Hell, sin, and death.

### Paragraph 104

But the belt or girdle of Christ is not set in the customary place, namely, about the loins. For as Arethas has also admonished, there are no desires to be restrained in Christ. Therefore, he is not girded after the manner of sinners, but about the breasts: so that we should understand by the girding that he is king of kings, void of all affections; most righteous and holy in judgments and government. But yet, in the meantime, he is furnished for the defense of his church, as we have read it written in Psalm 9. The Lord has put on strength and girded himself, and so forth. Christ might seem to have girded himself not after the manner that priests or kings use, for that he has obtained a more excellent priesthood and kingdom, enduring forever. To accomplish these things, it behooved him to use a temple, and a palace not transitory, but heaven itself. Hebrews 6 and 9. Yet, in the meantime, the effect penetrates into the church itself, so that he may be present in the church also.

### Paragraph 105

But the head of Christ appears gray, and his hair white, like the finest wool and the whitest snow. Such a head is also ascribed to the Father of our Lord Jesus Christ, as in the seventh chapter of Daniel. For they are of the same essence. And hereby is signified wisdom and age, and also the divinity and deity of Christ. And because Christ is God, therefore he is the head of the church, ministering to the body life, spirit, wisdom, and all celestial gifts. Ephesians 5. The Pope of Rome, that most wicked man of sin, does what? What a head he is—without life, without brains, utterly foolish. As he is described in the eleventh chapter of Zechariah. It is a shame that we will not see these things, being ever blind. Christ is everlasting, omnipotent, and knows all things. And he may be the health and head of the body. “In the beginning was the Word, and the Word was with God,” says Jerome. Christ himself: “Before Abraham was, I am,” says he. Therefore, the heretics lie, denying Christ to be very God, of the same substance with the Father. He is the dominion of God; all things are subject to him. Ephesians 1. And he himself fulfills all things, after the same sort being present with his church.

### Paragraph 106

Now his eyes are not darkened nor blind, but clear and bright. For Christ knows all things. Christ’s eyes are watchful; nothing is hidden from him; he sees all things that are done, both good and evil. And he sees in order that he may judge and require. He is light in darkness, and the sight of Christ is to good men joyful in perils. Finally, the judgments of Christ are righteous. The prophet David says, “The eyes of the Lord are upon the just, and his ears are to their prayer.” Again, “The face of the Lord is against those who do evil.” And just as the head is not plucked from the body, so Christ cannot be absent from his church. And considering that his eyes are quicksighted, and that the Lord foresees all our things, and has charge over us, how is he absent from his church? What need is there of any vicar?

### Paragraph 107

The feasts of the Lord are like copper, or like brass and frankincense burning in a furnace. For Chalcolibaum is a word compounded of brass and frankincense. This Erasmus notes, and that Swidas also shows that there is a kind of copper more precious than gold: which he says is made of saltpeter and of a stone. Pliny, in the thirty-fourth and second chapter, calls it a kind of brass, which in past times was highly valued and dug out of the veins of the earth. It seems to me to be the same which in the first and tenth of Ezekiel is called Hasmal, a present remedy against poisons. For if wine is poured into it, it will hiss. And so death and poison are detected.

### Paragraph 108

The most blatant, brazen, and fiery spectacle signifies the conduct and ways of the Lord, blameless, his judgments right and just.
